    Lead

    Lead, a metallic chemical element; its symbol is Pb (from the Lat. plumbum), and atomic weight 207.10 (O = 16). This metal was known to the ancients, and is mentioned in the Old Testament. The Romans used it largely, as it is still used, for the making of water pipes, and soldered these with an alloy of lead and tin. Pliny treats of these two metals as plumbum nigrum and plumbum album respectively, which seems to show that at his time they were looked upon as being only two varieties of the same species. In regard to the ancients' knowledge of lead compounds, we may state that the substance described by Dioscorides as μολυβδαίηα was undoubtedly litharge, that Pliny uses the word minium in its present sense of red lead, and that white lead was well known to Geber in the 8th century. The alchemists designated it by the sign of Saturn ♄.

    Toxicology

    Chronic - Critical

    Lead Poisoning, or Plumbism, a "disease of occupations," which is itself the cause of organic disease, particularly of the nervous and urinary systems. The workpeople affected are principally those engaged in potteries where lead glaze is used; but other industries in which health is similarly affected are file making, house painting and glazing, glass making, copper working, coach making, plumbing and gasfitting, printing, cutlery, and generally those occupations in which lead is concerned.

    The symptoms of chronic lead poisoning vary within very wide limits, from colic and constipation up to total blindness, paralysis, convulsions and death. They are thus described by Dr J. T. Arlidge (Diseases of Occupations):

    The poison finds its way gradually into the whole mass of the circulating blood, and exerts its effects mainly on the nervous system, paralysing nerve force and with it muscular power. Its victims become of a sallow waxy hue; the functions of the stomach and bowels are deranged, appetite fails and painful colic with constipation supervenes. The loss of power is generally shown first in the fingers, hands and wrists, and the condition known as "wrist drop" soon follows, rendering the victim useless for work. The palsy will extend to the shoulders, and after no long time to the legs also. Other organs frequently involved are the kidneys, the tissue of which becomes permanently damaged; whilst the sight is weakened or even lost.

    Dr M'Aldowie, senior physician to the North Staffordshire Infirmary, has stated that "in the pottery trade lead is very slow in producing serious effects compared with certain other industries." In his experience the average period of working in lead before serious lesions manifest themselves is 18 years for females and 22.5 years for males. But some individuals fall victims to the worst forms of plumbism after a few months' or even weeks' exposure to the danger. Young persons are more readily affected than those of mature age, and women more than men. In addition, there seems to be an element of personal susceptibility, the nature of which is not understood. Some persons "work in the lead" for twenty, forty or fifty years without the slightest ill effects; others have attacks whenever they are brought into contact with it. Possibly the difference is due to the general state of health; robust persons resist the poison successfully, those with impoverished blood and feeble constitution are mastered by it. Lead enters the body chiefly through the nose and mouth, being inspired in the form of dust or swallowed with food eaten with unwashed hands. It is very apt to get under the nails, and is possibly absorbed in this way through the skin. Personal care and cleanliness are therefore of the greatest importance. A factory surgeon of great experience in the English Potteries has stated that seventeen out of twenty cases of lead poisoning in the china and earthenware industry are due to carelessness (The Times, 8th October 1898).

    The Home Office in England has from time to time made special rules for workshops and workpeople, with the object of minimizing or preventing the occurrence of lead poisoning; and in 1895 notification of cases was made compulsory. The health of workpeople in the Potteries was the subject of a special inquiry by a scientific committee in 1893. The committee stated that "the general truth that the potteries occupation is one fraught with injury to health and life is beyond dispute," and that "the ill effects of the trade are referable to two chief causes — namely, dust and the poison of lead." Of these the inhalation of clay and flint dust was the more important. It led to bronchitis, pulmonary tuberculosis and pneumonia, which were the most prevalent disorders among potters, and responsible for 70% of the mortality. That from lead the committee did not attempt to estimate, but they found that plumbism was less prevalent than in past times, and expressed the opinion "that a large part of the mortality from lead poisoning is avoidable; although it must always be borne in mind that no arrangements or rules, with regard to the work itself, can entirely obviate the effects of the poison to which workers are exposed, because so much depends upon the individual and the observance of personal care and cleanliness." They recommended the adoption of certain special rules in the workshops, with the objects of protecting young persons from the lead, of minimizing the evils of dust, and of promoting cleanliness, particularly in regard to meals. Some of these recommendations were adopted and applied with good results. With regard to the suggestion that "only leadless glazes should be used on earthenware," they did not "see any immediate prospect of such glazes becoming universally applicable to pottery manufacture," and therefore turned their attention to the question of "fritting" the lead.

    It may be explained that lead is used in china and earthenware to give the external glaze which renders the naturally porous ware watertight. Both "white" and "red" lead are used. The lead is added to other ingredients, which have been "fritted" or fused together and then ground very fine in water, making a thick creamy liquid into which the articles are dipped. After dipping the glaze dries quickly, and on being "fired" in the kiln it becomes fused by the heat into the familiar glassy surface. In the manufacture of ware with enamelled colours, glaze is mixed with the pigment to form a flux, and such colours are used either moist or in the form of a dry powder. "Fritting" the lead means mixing it with the other ingredients of the glaze beforehand and fusing them all together under great heat into a kind of rough glass, which is then ground to make the glaze. Treated in this way the lead combines with the other ingredients and becomes less soluble, and therefore less dangerous, than when added afterwards in the raw state. The committee (1893) thought it "reasonable to suppose that the fritting of lead might ultimately be found universally practicable," but declared that though fritting "no doubt diminishes the danger of lead poisoning," they "could not regard all fritts as equally innocuous."

    In the annual report of the chief inspector of factories for 1897, it was stated that there had been "material improvement in dust conditions" in the potting industry, but "of lead poisoning unfortunately the same could not be said, the number of grave cases reported, and particularly cases of blindness, having ominously increased of late." This appears to have been largely due to the erroneous inclusion among potting processes of "litho transfer making," a colour industry in which girls are employed. New special rules were imposed in 1899 prohibiting the employment of persons under fifteen in the dangerous processes, ordering a monthly examination of all women and young persons working in lead by the certifying surgeon, with power to suspend those showing symptoms of poisoning, and providing for the more effectual removal of dust and the better enforcement of cleanliness. At the same time a scientific inquiry was ordered into the practicability of dispensing with lead in glazes or of substituting fritted compounds for the raw carbonate. The scientific experts reported in 1899, recommending that the use of raw lead should be absolutely prohibited, and expressing the opinion that the greater amount of earthenware could be successfully glazed without any lead. These views were in advance of the opinions held by practical potters, and met with a good deal of opposition. By certain manufacturers considerable progress had been made in diminishing the use of raw lead and towards the discovery of satisfactory leadless glazes; but it is a long step from individual experiments to the wholesale compulsory revolution of the processes of manufacture in so large and varied an industry, and in the face of foreign competitors hampered by no such regulations. The materials used by each manufacturer have been arrived at by a long process of experience, and they are such as to suit the particular goods he supplies for his particular market. It is therefore difficult to apply a uniform rule without jeopardizing the prosperity of the industry, which supports a population of 250,000 in the Potteries alone. However, the bulk of the manufacturers agreed to give up the use of raw lead, and to fritt all their glazes in future, time being allowed to effect the change of process; but they declined to be bound to any particular composition of glaze for the reasons indicated.

    In 1901 the Home Office brought forward a new set of special rules. Most of these were framed to strengthen the provisions for securing cleanliness, removing dust, &c., and were accepted with a few modifications. But the question of making even more stringent regulations, even to the extent of making the use of lead glaze illegal altogether, was still agitated; and in 1906 the Home Office again appointed an expert committee to reinvestigate the subject. They reported in 1910, and made various recommendations in detail for strengthening the existing regulations; but while encouraging the use of leadless glaze in certain sorts of common ceramic ware, they pointed out that, without the use of lead, certain other sorts could either not be made at all or only at a cost or sacrifice of quality which would entail the loss of important markets.

    In 1908 Dr Collis made an inquiry into the increase of plumbism in connexion with the smelting of metals, and he considered the increase in the cases of poisoning reported to be due to the third schedule of the Workmen's Compensation Act, (1) by causing the prevalence of pre-existing plumbism to come to light, (2) by the tendency this fostered to replace men suspected of lead impregnation by new hands amongst whom the incidence is necessarily greater.

    Chronic

    Lead damages1 the endothelial cells that line blood vessels. This hardens arteries and causes plaque to form. Lead increases2 blood pressure, in part by disturbing the calcium messenger system regulation of blood pressure. Lead is found in the soil because of past use of leaded gasoline, water can have lead because old plumbing solder used lead, old paint sometimes has lead.

    Ailments

    • Cardiovascular - Atherosclerotic plaque1
    • Cardiovascular - Hypertension (High blood pressure)2

    Bibliography

    1. L van Strijp et al., Investigating the effect of the heavy metals cadmium, chromium and lead, alone and in combination on an endothelial cell line (March 2023)
    2. J Schwartz, Lead, blood pressure, and cardiovascular disease in men (Jan. 1995)

    Lithium

    Compounds

    Lithium orotate

    Treatment
    Ailments

    Hans Nieper found1 that lithium orotate in doses of 138 mg x 5 times per week treated cases of depression, headaches and migraine, epilepsy, and alcoholism. He reported that 5 mg of lithium orotate was roughly as effective as 100 mg of lithium carbonate. Because the lithium dose from lithium orotate treatment is a small fraction of the lithium from lithium carbonate treatment, there is no risk of kidney toxicity associated with high dose lithium carbonate.

    • Brain - Headache
    • Brain - Migraine
    • Brain - Neurosis - Depression
    • Brain - Psychosis - Epilepsy / Seizure
    • Brain - Psychosis - Manic behavior
    Improvements
    • Cardiovascular - Cardiovascular function
    • Liver - Liver function

    • Thyroid - Thyroid function

    Lysine

    L-lysine is1 as an essential amino acid. Animal proteins including meats, poultry, and milk have high amounts of lysine. Among vegan foods, wheat germ also contains large amounts of lysine. Lysine is glycogenic and ketogenic. Lysine is2 required for the production of collagen, growth and development, fertily, digestive health, hormone production, organ health, antioxidant activity, methylation, detoxification, absorption of important minerals (calcium, iron and zinc), pain relief, and immune function, especially1 antiviral activity.

    Lysine deficiency is a contributing cause of angina1 3, narrowing of the arteries1 3, osteoporosis1, thrombosis1 3, and viral diseases, especially herpes1 4. Supplemental lysine is typically in the form of lysine hydrochloride. Fortification of animal feeds with lysine has been common since 1970. The minimum adult requirement for lysine is5 estimated at 12 mg/day/kg, 840 mg/day for a 70 kg person.

    Arginine and lysine are2 antagonists. Fermentation of high arginine grains increases lysine in the diet. Traditionally; wheat, rye, millet, rice and quinoa were ground then fermented for many hours, resulting in items like sourdough bread, South Indian rice pancakes and African teff or millet breads. In modern times, grains used to make bread, crackers, pizza, pasta and desserts undergo little or no fermentation. Legumes and grains contain trypsin in­hibitors, which block absorption of lysine, and modern wheat has been bred to have even more trypsin inhibitors to protect against insects. Trypsin inhibitors are broken down during traditional fermentation. Therefore; modern unfermented wheat has less lysine and more trypsin inhibitors than traditional wheat, making for far less absorbed lysine. Gossypol, a poison in cottonseed oil, depletes lysine, it may be present in cows and farmed fish, as both are commonly fed cottonseed meal. Because modern food is deficient in lysine and high in lysine inhibitors, lysine deficiency is common. Vegans and people exercising vigorously have a higher risk of lysine deficiency.

    Biology

    Lysine helps1 in the formation of D-glucose, glycogen, lipids, and connective tissues. Lysine and leucine are the only two amino acids that break down into ketones rather than glucose. Ly­sine also forms glycine when it is converted to carnitine. Carnitine is a form of the ketone beta-hydroxybutyrate, and is a ketosis inducer. Lysine activates PPAR alpha, which induces ketogen­esis. Lysine increases diabetics’ insulin receptor enzyme activity, which lowers their blood sugar. Lysine maintains functional hemoglobin by preventing hemo­globin glycation (high HbA1c). Lysine increases glutathione, cata­lase and superoxide dismutase.

    Lysine activates vitamin D3, which increases calcium absorption. Lysine also regulates calcium through the cholesterol trans­porter NPC1, which also is needed for autophagy. Lysine is needed to terminate autophagy and begin the regeneration pro­cess. Lysine dissolves kidney calcium oxalate crystals (which are the most common type of kidney stones), and it prevents calcification of the arteries.

    Lysine helps the body absorb iron, so a lysine deficiency can lead to a low iron level, which can cause fatigue and anemia. Lysine increases natural killer (NK) cell activity. Lysine is needed to inhibit inflammatory tumor necrosis factor (TNF) and interferon. Lysine is needed for the longevity protein SIRT1. Lysine is needed for the neu­rotransmitter GABA, and to activate myelin repair. Methylation of lysine activates the major cancer-fighting protein p53. Lysine (as acetyl coenzyme A) is a component of acetylcholine.

    When lysine is absorbed into lipoprotein(a) (Lp(a))’s lysine-binding site, it becomes normal low density lipoprotein (LDL). Lysine converts cholesterol into bile, thus controlling cholesterol and preventing fatty liver.

    Most of the arginine present in the body is produced in the body (endogenous), as needed from citrulline with the help of lysine. Arginine and lysine are dietary antagonists, and lysine reduces the harmful effects of dietary or supplementary (exogenous) arginine. Exces­sive exogenous arginine supports pathogens as well as cancer. Arginine releases nitric oxide (NO) into the blood, and in an inflammatory situation, exces­sive arginine increases inflammatory nitric oxide. Nitric oxide has many important functions in the body, but inflammatory nitric oxide, with its harmful and reactive free radical peroxynitrite, can cause delayed muscle soreness following exercise, kidney damage, high homocysteine, low glutathione and even death. A high arginine diet may interfere with the kidneys’ disposal of uric acid. Excess arginine increases the antidiuretic hormone arginine vasopressin (AVP), which raises blood pressure by retaining water.

    Premature infants, who cannot synthesize arginine, need slightly more arginine than lysine in order to produce growth hormone. Full term infants need more lysine, and mother’s milk is higher in lysine than arginine.

    Angina

    Angina, narrowing of the arteries in general, and thrombosis are1 3 caused by plaque. Lysyl hydroxylase is an enzyme responsible for connecting lysine residues on adjacent collagen strands. Lysyl hydroxylase requires vitamin C, a vitamin C deficiency leads to insufficient lysyl hydroxylase, which disrupts lysine crosslinking, which weakens collagen strands. When collagen is weak it cracks, and if there is a lysine and/or proline deficiency, the exposed lysine strands from the crack bind to circulating lipoprotein (a), a cholesterol that has lysine receptors. The attachment of lipoprotein(a) to the free lysine residues of damaged collagen builds plaque deposits which eventually narrow the artery obstructing blood flow, or the plaques rupture leading to thrombosis, a blood clot inside a blood vessel, which also obstructs blood flow.

    The standard Pauling Protocol to treat angina is 3 g of vitamin C, 2.5 g of lysine, and 1.25 g of proline, taken twice daily with food. The total dose per day is 6 g of vitamin C, 5 g of lysine, and 2.5 g of proline. Also, 300 mg of annatto vitamin E should be taken once per day. The fast Pauling Protocol to treat angina quickly is 2 g of vitamin C, 2 g of lysine, 1 g of proline, taken twice daily away from food. The total dose per day is 4 g of vitamin C, 4 g of lysine, and 2 g of proline. Also, 300 mg of annatto vitamin E should be taken once per day.

    Osteoporosis

    Lysine increases the absorption of calcium. A study found6 that women with osteoporosis taking 400 mg/day of lysine decreased their excretion of calcium. Another study found that women with osteoporosis taking 800 mg/day of lysine increased their absorption of calcium.

    Herpes

    Many studies found that people with herpes on a high lysine low arginine diet had fewer lesions when supplementing with lysine. A study tested4 a 500 mg/day dose and a 1,000 mg/day dose, it found that the large dose decreased the recurrence rate of herpes simplex attacks and the small dose was ineffective. The studies were done with high lysine diets, therefore with a normal diet, the required lysine dose will be higher, 3 g/day seems reasonable given that doses up to 3 g/day are well tolerated.

    Treatment

    For angina or narrowing of the arteries, the Pauling Protocol should be used, for herpes 3 g/day should be used, the typical dose for most other ailments is 1 g/day. Ailments which lysine helps with follow below.

    Magnesium

    Compounds

    Magnesium Malate

    Treatment

    Malic acid can1 remove aluminum from the body. Magnesium malate is a common malic salt and provides needed magnesium, and so is one of the safest and most effective ways of removing aluminum. Excess aluminum is common and especially dangerous in people who have2 been injected with vaccines containing aluminum adjuvants. Aluminum causes many illnesses, especially those of the brain.

    Ailments
    • Brain - Dementia - Alzheimer's disease
    • Brain - Neurodevelopmental disorder - Attention deficit/hyperactivity disorder (ADHD)
    • Brain - Neurodevelopmental disorder - Autism

    Magnesium Orotate

    Treatment

    Hans Nieper found3 that a dose of 380 mg/day improved the elasticity of blood vessels. He used between 1 and 2.5 g/day to clear arteries. Potassium orotate was often given with magnesium orotate in a roughly 1:9 ratio. A reasonable rounded dose would be 100 mg/day of potassium orotate with 1 g/day of magnesium orotate to 300 mg/day of potassium orotate with 2.5 g/day of magnesium orotate. When Nieper gave his patients bromelain, and 1.5 to 2.5 g/day of magnesium orotate with a proportional amount of potassium orotate, their 2 and 4 year mortality rates were reduced by 90% compared to conventional treatment. Magnesium orotate should treat every ailment that other magnesium compounds treat with fewer adverse effects, but this has not been tested for most ailments because the other compounds are more popular.

    Ailments
    • Cardiovascular - Arterial calcification
    • Cardiovascular - Arteriosclerosis
    Improvements

    Magnesium orotate also increases athletic performance. Triathletes were tested after 4 weeks of supplementation with placebo or magnesium orotate. Blood was collected before and after a course consisting of a 380-meter swim, a 20-km bicycle race, and a 5-km run. Compared to placebo, magnesium orotate caused a greater increase in serum glucose and venous partial pressure of oxygen, and a greater decrease in serum insulin, blood acidity, and serum cortisol. The athletes had greater endurance with magnesium orotate.

    • Endurance

    Magnesium Threonate

    Treatment

    Magnesium threonate transports4 magnesium through the blood–brain barrier, making it more effective for illnesses of the central nervous system (CNS). The typical dose is 2 g/day. It can reduce pain and partly reverse5 Alzheimer's disease.

    Ailments
    • Brain - Dementia - Alzheimer's disease
    • Gastrointestinal disorder - Constipation
    • Pain

    Mercury

    Mercury (symbol Hg, atomic weight = 200), in chemistry, a metallic element which is easily distinguished from all others by its being liquid at even the lowest temperatures naturally occurring in moderate climates. To this exceptional property it owes the synonyms of quicksilver in English (with the Germans Quecksilber is the only recognized name) and of hydrargyrum (from υδωρ, water, and άργυρος, silver) in Graeco-Latin. This metal does not appear to have been known to the ancient Jews, nor is it mentioned by the earlier Greek writers. Theophrastus (about 300 BC) mentions it as prepared from cinnabar by treatment with copper and vinegar. Dioscorides obtained it from the same mineral with the aid of iron, employing at the same time a primitive distillation apparatus. With the alchemists it was a substance of great consequence. Its appearance commended it as a substance for investigation; many of its compounds, especially corrosive sublimate and calomel, were studied, and improved methods for extracting and purifying the metal were devised. Being ignorant of its susceptibility of freezing into a compact solid, they did not recognize it as a true metal, and yet, on the authority of Geber, they held that mercury (meaning the predominating element in this metal) enters into the composition of all metals, and is the very cause of their metallicity (see ELEMENT). When, about the beginning of the 16th century, chemistry and scientific medicine came to merge into one, this same mysterious element of "mercury" played a great part in the theories of pathology; and the metal, in the free as in certain combined states, came to be looked upon as a powerful medicinal agent.

    Toxicology

    Acute

    Acute poisoning by mercurials usually occurs in the case of corrosive sublimate. There is intense gastrointestinal inflammation, with vomiting, frequent "rice water" stools and extreme collapse. The treatment, except when the case is seen at once, is very difficult, but white of egg or other form of albumen is the antidote, forming an insoluble compound with the perchloride.

    Chronic - Critical

    Chronic poisoning (hydrargyrism or mercurialism) is of great importance, since any indication of its symptoms must be closely watched for in patients who are under mercurial treatment. Usually the first symptom is slight tenderness of the teeth whilst eating, and some foetor of the breath. These symptoms become more marked and the gums become the seat of severe inflammation, being spongy, vascular and prone to bleed. The salivary glands are swollen and tender, and the saliva pours from the mouth, and may amount to pints in the course of a day. The teeth become quite loose and may fall out. The symptoms are aggravated until the tongue and mouth ulcerate, the jaw bone necroses, haemorrhages occur in various parts of the body, and the patient dies of anaemia, septic inflammation or exhaustion. The treatment consists, besides stopping the intake of poison and relieving the symptoms, in the administration of potassium iodide in small, often repeated doses.

    Chronic

    Mercury is used in gold amalgamation processes and the concentration of mercury in people and seafood in a gold mining area were1 measured. The most frequent symptoms documented were headache, muscle cramps, cough, and aphthous ulcers. Sometimes there is a delay between exposure to methylmercury and the onset2 of neurodegenerative symptoms. Mercury increases3 production of free radicals and also disables antioxidant defenses, including4 glutathione.

    Mercury activates5 the enzyme phospholipase D (PLD). The enzyme in turn triggers the release of phosphatidic acid, which damages endothelial cells in the blood vessel lining, which leads to vascular disorders. Mercury induces thrombosis, partly by changing the shape of platelets. Mercury induced inflammation alters6 arachidonic acid metabolites, increasing the porosity of the vascular endothelium. Vascular smooth muscle cells (VSMCs) help regulate blood pressure, mercury alters them and therefore increases the risk of hypertension. Mercury induces dyslipidemia, immune dsyfunction and mitochondrial dsyfunction.

    Mercury is in dental amalgalm, some seafood and it is used in gold amalgamation processes. Some vaccines contain the mercury compound thiomersal.

    Ailments
    • Brain - Headache1
    • Cardiovascular - Dyslipidemia6
    • Cardiovascular - Hypertension (High blood pressure)6
    • Cardiovascular - Thrombosis5
    • Immune dysfunction6
    • Mitochondrial dsyfunction6
    • Musculoskeletal - Muscle cramps1
    • Ulcer - Aphthous ulcers1
    • Vascular disorders5

    N-Acetyl Cysteine (NAC)

    N-acetyl cysteine (NAC) is1 (2R)-2-acetamido-3-sulfanylpropanoic acid (C5H9 NO3S), a thiol compound which is an N-acetyl derivative of the amino acid L-cysteine. It is a white crystalline powder with a sour taste and slight acetic odor. There is high first pass metabolism and half of the drug is bound to plasma proteins. It was2 developed to treat paracetamol overdose and loosen mucus. It is3 found in onions, and cysteine is4 in foods including chicken, turkey garlic, yogurt, and eggs. Taken orally, deacetylation of NAC to form cysteine happens while passing along the small intestine and liver, reducing its bioavailability to around 6%. NAC attenuates aminophylline-induced seizures but aggravates5 absence seizures.

    Biology

    NAC stimulates glutathione biosynthesis, enhances glutathione S-transferase activity, promotes detoxification, moderates clinging mucous secretions, decreases mitochondrial membrane depolarization and scavenges free radicals. NAC protects against toxic chemicals because it is a nucleophile and a -sulfahydryl (–SH group) donor.

    Treatment

    Ailment - Critical

    The dose to prevent an acetaminophen overdose is2 140 mg/kg.

    • Toxicity - Acetaminophen toxicity6

    Side Effects

    Asthmatics should not inhale2 in NAC, because it can cause a bronchospasm. NAC slows blood clotting, so someone going into surgery or who has a bleeding disorder should not take NAC.

    NAC can cause nausea, vomiting, constipation, diarrhea, chest tightness, drowsiness, swelling of the mouth, clamminess, and a runny nose. Rarely, it can cause rashes, headache, low blood pressure, fever, and liver problems. Very rarely, it can cause an allergic reaction, which may lead to a nonimmune type of anaphylaxis.

    Interactions

    NAC increases the effects of nitroglycerin, increasing the dilation of blood vessels. NAC taken with nitroglycerin could cause lightheadedness, headache, and dizziness.

    Activated charcoal is often used when people have been overdosed, and so is NAC. Unfortunately, if you combine charcoal and NAC, the charcoal will bind with the NAC instead of the poison, stopping both treatments from working. If someone has been overdosed, NAC or charcoal should be used, not both at the same time.

    N-Acetyl Cysteine Ethyl Ester (NACET)

    N-acetyl cysteine ethyl ester (NACET) is1 a lipophilic form of N-acetyl cysteine (NAC). NACET is a white powder with a melting point of 44°C. It is soluble in water and organic solvents. It enters cells where it is transformed into NAC and cysteine. NACET has2 a much higher bioavailability than NAC. Due to its sulfur content, NACET can cause an unpleasant odor.

    Biology

    NACET can be synthesized under argon atmosphere by N-acetylation of L-cysteine ethyl ester in dichloromethane with equimolar amounts of acetic anhydride.

    Treatment

    The bioavailability of NACET is high and too much NACET actually reduces glutathione synthesis, so the optimal NACET dose is small, typically 50 mg/day.

    1. Daniela Giustarini et al., N-Acetylcysteine ethyl ester (NACET): A novel lipophilic cell-permeable cysteine derivative with an unusual pharmacokinetic feature and remarkable antioxidant potential (2012 Dec.)
    2. Daniela Giustarini et al., N-acetylcysteine ethyl ester as GSH enhancer in human primary endothelial cells: a comparative study with other drugs (2018 Aug.)

    Nose Hair Trimming

    Nose hair filters particles, protecting people from air pollution and respiratory allergies. Increased nasal hair density improves the filtering efficiency of the nose, while reduced nasal hair density decreases its efficiency. A study found1 that people with seasonal rhinitis with many nose hairs had a 17% rate of asthma, and those with few nose hairs had a 45% rate of asthma.

    Nose hair can grow out of the nose and be visible. This nose hair can be trimmed or plucked. Nose hair can be safely trimmed with a round tip nose hair tweezer trimmer or an electric razor attachment. Trimming removes hair outside the nose, where it is visible and of little use, and leaves hair inside the nose, where it is filtering particles. Plucking2 nose hair damages hair follicles and can create open wounds in the nostrils, increasing the risk of infection, including folliculitis or an abscess. An infection can cause swelling, irritation, and fever. Also because plucking removes nose hairs, it reduces nasal hair density and therefore increases the risk of respiratory allergies.

    Round Tip Nose Hair Trimming Tweezer

    A round tip nose hair trimming tweezer is3 a tweezer with a ring clip at one end and a round head at the other. When squeezed together, the head cuts hair against the inside of the ring clip. Since the ring cutting surface is on the inside, it is safer than trimming with scissors which could accidentally cut the skin since they have cutting action at their tip. The tweezer should be made of stainless steel.

    Ailments

    Ailments which nasal hairs prevent follow below.

    • Airborne pollution4
    • Immune dysfunction - Autoimmune disease - Pet sensitivities4
    • Immune dysfunction - Autoimmune disease - Seasonal allergies4
    • Respiratory disease - Asthma1
    • Respiratory infections4

    Orotic Acid

    Orotic acid (OA) C5H4N2O4, is1 a pyrimidine carboxylic acid produced as an intermediate in the biosynthesis of pyrimidine nucleotides. Orotic acid is not a true vitamin, but orotic acid and its derivative, uridine monophosphate (UMP), are sometimes referred to collectively as vitamin B13. Orotic acid is found in small amounts in foods including dairy products, organ meats, and some grains including barley and oats. The typical supplementation dose is2 500 mg/day.

    Orotic acid increases the production of ribose moieties (R-1-P and R-5-P), leading to increased production of adenosine triphosphate (ATP). Orotic acid increases the pool of Uridine Monophosphate/Cytidine Monophosphate (UMP/CMP), reducing the demand for ATP to synthesize them, therefore increasing the amount of available ATP. Orotic acid increases intracellular glycogen stores by increasing glucose disposal into muscle. Its molecular weight is3 156 g/mol. Orotate phosphoribosyltransferase (OPRTase) is responsible for catalyzing the first step in the conversion of orotic acid into uridine, by facilitating the attachment of a ribose plus phosphate group to orotic acid. The result is the formation of orotidine 5'-monophosphate (OMP), which is the precursor to UMP.

    Orotates

    When orotic acid reacts with a metal, an orotate salt is produced. Orotate salts have a higher bioavailability than any other salts. For example calcium orotate has4 a bioavailability of at least 90%, while the next highest bioavailability is 45% for calcium aspartate. Because of this, a smaller amount of mineral is required in the supplement for the body to absorb the required dose, so adverse effects are minimized. Common orotates follow below.

    • Calcium orotate
    • Lithium orotate
    • Magnesium orotate
    • Potassium orotate
    • Zinc orotate

    Treatment

    The typical supplementation dose is2 500 mg/day. Because orotate salts have the advantage of carrying their metal into the cells of the body, taking orotic acid as a salt gives a double benefit. A common orotate is magnesium orotate. A common combination is5 potassium orotate with magnesium orotate in a 1:9 ratio. A reasonable rounded dose would be 100 mg/day of potassium orotate with 1 g/day of magnesium orotate to 300 mg/day of potassium orotate with 2.5 g/day of magnesium orotate. When Hans Nieper gave his patients bromelain, and 1.5 to 2.5 g/day of magnesium orotate with a proportional amount of potassium orotate, their 2 and 4 year mortality rates were reduced by 90% compared to conventional treatment.

    Ailments

    • Cardiovascular - Ischemia6
    • Immune dysfunction - Autoimmune disease - Lupus - Lupus erythematosus5
    • Heart - Myocardial hypertrophy7
    • Neurodegenerative disease - Multiple sclerosis (MS)5

    Improvements

    • Endurance2

    Pauling Protocol

    Standard

    The standard Pauling Protocol is1 3 g of vitamin C, 2.5 g of lysine, and 1.25 g of proline, taken twice daily with food. The total dose per day is 6 g of vitamin C, 5 g of lysine, and 2.5 g of proline. Also, 300 mg of annatto vitamin E should be taken once per day.

    Lysyl hydroxylase is an enzyme responsible for connecting lysine residues on adjacent collagen strands. Lysyl hydroxylase requires vitamin C, a vitamin C deficiency leads to insufficient lysyl hydroxylase, which disrupts lysine crosslinking, which weakens collagen strands. When collagen is weak it cracks, and if there is a lysine and/or proline deficiency, the exposed lysine strands from the crack bind to circulating lipoprotein (a), a cholesterol that has lysine receptors. The attachment of lipoprotein(a) to the free lysine residues of damaged collagen builds plaque deposits which eventually narrow the artery obstructing blood flow, or the plaques rupture leading to thrombosis, a blood clot inside a blood vessel, which also obstructs blood flow. Annatto vitamin E inhibits proliferation of smooth muscle cells in the walls of arteries. Of all natural sources, annatto vitamin E has the highest concentration of delta-tocotrienol, which is the most effective form of vitamin E. Linus Pauling and Matthias Rath discovered that a lack of vitamin C, lysine and proline lead to heart disease. Pauling recommended2 supplemention vitamin C and lysine to someone dying of angina, and in two months the angina was mostly cured. Barrie Tan discovered3 the delta-tocotrienol form of vitamin E in annatto.

    Fast

    The fast Pauling Protocol for clearing arteries is4 2 g of vitamin C and 2 g of lysine, taken twice daily away from food. The total dose per day is 4 g of vitamin C and 4 g of lysine. Away from food means no food for a minimum of 2 hours before the supplements and one hour afterwards. Taking those supplements away from food increases their absorption, which speeds the dissolution of the plaque.

    This protocol can clear arteries in two weeks, it should be used if the heart disease is severe or if the standard protocol does not work. If a person has heart problems severe enough that surgery is recommended, they should try this first for a month and only have surgery if this protocol doesn't work.

    Vitamin C absorption is impaired by carbohydrates via glucose competition. lysine absorption is impaired by protein via amino competition. Vitamin C and lysine do not compete with each other and can be taken together. To maximize the time between the supplements and food, the supplements should be taken when waking up and just before going to bed. An example schedule for someone who wakes up at 7 AM and goes to bed at 10 PM is vitamin C and lysine at 7 AM, food between 9 AM and 7 PM, vitamin C and lysine at 10 PM. If someone has problems taking vitamin C or lysine on an empty stomach, an example schedule is 2,000 mg vitamin C with a small protein meal without carbohydrates at 7 AM, lysine with a small carbohydrate meal without protein at 10 AM, food between 12 AM and 4 PM, 2,000 mg vitamin C with a small protein meal without carbohydrates at 7 PM, 2,000 mg lysine with a small carbohydrate meal without protein at 10 PM.

    An option to increase the effectiveness of the fast protocol is to add proline, and annatto vitamin E. In the standard protocol, the proline dose is half of the lysine dose. In the fast protocol 2 g of lysine is taken at a time, so 1 g of proline should be taken with the 2 g of vitamin C and 2 g of lysine, twice per day. The total dose per day would be 4 g of vitamin C, 4 g of lysine and 2 g of proline. Also, 300 mg of annatto vitamin E should be taken once per day.

    Potassium

    Potassium [symbol K (from kalium), atomic weight 39.114 O=16)], a metallic chemical element, belonging to the group termed the metals of the alkalis. Although never found free in nature, in combination the metal is abundantly and widely distributed. Potassiferous silicates occur everywhere in the earth's crust; orthoclase (potash felspar) and potash mica may be quoted as prominent examples. Such potassiferous silicates are found in almost all rocks, both as normal and as accessory components; and their disintegration furnishes the soluble potassium salts which are found in all fertile soils. These salts are sucked up by the roots of plants, and by taking part in the process of nutrition are partly converted into oxalate, tartrate, and other organic salts, which, when the plants are burned, are converted into the carbonate, K2CO3. It is a remarkable fact that, although in a given soil the soda-content may predominate largely over the potash salts, the plants growing in the soil take up the latter: in the ashes of most land plants the potash (calculated as K2O) forms upwards of 90% of the total alkali. The proposition holds, in its general sense, for sea plants likewise. In ocean water the ratio of soda (Na2O) to potash (K2O) is 100:3-23 (Dittmar); in kelp it is, on the average, too: 5-26 (Richardson). Ashes particularly rich in potash are those of burning nettles, wormwood (Artemisia absinthium), tansy (Tanacetum vulgare), fumitory (Fumaria officinalis), and tobacco. In fact, the ashes of herbs generally are richer in potash than those of the trunks and branches of trees; yet, for obvious reasons, the latter are of greater industrial importance as sources of potassium carbonate. According to Liebig, potassium is the essential alkali of the animal body; and it may be noted that sheep excrete most of the potassium which they take from the land as sweat, one-third of the weight of raw merino consisting of potassium compounds.

    Compounds

    Potassium Orotate

    Treatment
    Ailments

    Potassium orotate can1 treat ailments that other potassium compounds can not. For example, potassium orotate prevents idiopathic myocardial necrosis in hamsters, while potassium chloride is ineffective. When administered to heart attack patients, potassium orotate resulted in faster recovery of myocardial contractibility than placebo controls. Hans Nieper used potassium orotate with magnesium orotate in a roughly 1:9 ratio to clear arteries. A reasonable rounded dose would be 100 mg/day of potassium orotate with 1 g/day of magnesium orotate to 300 mg/day of potassium orotate with 2.5 g/day of magnesium orotate. When Nieper gave his patients bromelain, and 1.5 to 2.5 g/day of magnesium orotate with a proportional amount of potassium orotate, their 2 and 4 year mortality rates were reduced by 90% compared to conventional treatment. Potassium orotate should treat every ailment that other potassium compounds treat with fewer adverse effects, but this has not been tested for most ailments because the other compounds are more popular.

    • Brain - Neurosis - Anxiety
    • Brain - Neurosis - Depression
    • Lethargy
    Recoveries

    Potassium orotate speeds wound healing, recovery of myocardial contractibility in heart attack patients, and recovery and immune function following surgery. Potassium orotate facilitates the tissue uptake of vitamin C from serum and increases blood levels of glutathione.

    Toxicology

    Poisoning by caustic potash may take place or poisoning by pearl ash containing caustic potash. A caustic taste in the mouth is quickly followed by burning abdominal pain, vomiting and diarrhoea, with a feeble pulse and a cold clammy skin; the post-mortem appearances are those of acute gastrointestinal irritation. The treatment is washing out the stomach or giving emetics followed by vinegar or lemon juice and later oil and white of egg.

    Proline

    L-proline and its metabolite (hydroxyproline) are1 amino acids which constitute one third of the amino acids in collagen proteins. Proline is involved in protein synthesis and structure, metabolism (particularly the synthesis of arginine, polyamines, and glutamate), wound healing, antioxidative reactions, and immune responses. Proline and hydroxyproline are most abundant in collagen and milk proteins. The most important use of proline is to remove arterial plaque in the Pauling Protocol.

    Red Light Therapy Helmet

    Low level laser therapy (LLLT) promotes1 hair growth in androgenetic alopecia (AGA). The wavelength is typically around 660 nm. The therapy light can also be from a light emitting diode (LED). A laser illuminates cells below the scalp better than an LED, because a laser has a lower beam divergence.

    People using a laser only red light therapy helmet, with a light dose of 4 J/cm2 three times a week for 24 weeks, grew more hair than the controls. The average hair density of the therapy group was 112 hairs/cm2 and increased by 10 hairs/cm2, 6 more than the control group, a percentage improvement of 5%. There was no significant difference in improvement between men and women. Men using2 a laser and LED red light therapy helmet, with a light dose of 67 J/cm2 every other day for 16 weeks, grew much more hair than the controls. The average hair density of the therapy group was 142 hairs/cm2 and increased by 87 hairs/cm2, 87 more than the control group, a percentage improvement of 61%. Note, the study had arithmetic errors, so the calculation was not from the study. The increased improvement may be because of the higher power, or because the helmet used laser and LED light, no study was found which compared the two types of light of the same energy.

    To grow as much hair as possible, the headgear should be a helmet extending over the back and sides. It should have vents, to keep the head cool. For convenience and safety, the helmet should be rechargeable, rather than needing to be plugged in. A cap has no room for ventilation because it is tight around the head, and it does not illuminate below the headband, so it not as good as good as a helmet. Red light therapy helmets with only laser light only include the Theradome EVO LH403 Theradome PRO LH804 . Red light therapy helmets with laser and LED light include the iRESTORE Essential5 , iRESTORE Professional6 and iRESTORE Elite7 . The Theradome8 helmets have a built in battery and the iRESTORE9 helmets have a battery option.

    Ailments

    • Hair - Hair loss (Androgenetic alopecia) (AGA)

    A rigid razor like the Henson shaver supports the blade close to the cutting edge, reducing blade bending.     Sepsis

    Sepsis (Gr. δηψις, putrefaction), or Septic Infection, a term applied in medicine and surgery to indicate the resultant infection of a wound or sore by microorganisms or by their products. Under this general heading come three great constitutional diseases, differing radically from each other in their aetiology and pathology: sapraemia, septicaemia and pyaemia.

    The modern standard Intensive Care Unit (ICU) care and the life saving Marik Protocol which is built upon that are in the septic shock article.

    Sapraemia

    Sapraemia (Gr. δαπρός, rotten, αίμα, blood), or septic intoxication, is the result of the absorption of a dose of the toxins produced by microorganisms from some area of infection without the entrance of the microorganisms themselves into the blood. This condition was for a long time confounded with septicaemia, but is distinguished from it in being a chemical intoxication. The blood in sapraemia if injected into an animal is incapable of reproducing the disease as in septicaemia. Any condition in which there is a mass of decomposing tissue in the neighbourhood of an unhealed wound may give rise to sapraemia. In surgical practice it may be met with in large, deep and badly drained wounds where a quantity of putrifying material is pent up. When it arises in connexion with wounds accidentally received, it may be unavoidably due to the dirty state of the skin or to foreign bodies entering the wound. Absorption of toxins is notably frequent in portions of decomposing placental tissue which may accidentally have remained behind in the uterus after childbirth, and may give rise to puerperal sapraemia. Sapraemia is acute or subacute directly according to the amount of toxin absorbed. By some writers it is divided as follows: (1) Hectic fever is a chronic blood poisoning with continual absorption of small doses of the toxins. This variety usually arises in long continued suppuration of bones and joints, and in decomposition occurring in a pulmonary cavity. The marked symptom is a sharp rise of temperature in the evenings; the face becomes flushed and the pulse rapid. After profuse sweating the temperature drops. Diarrhoea and wasting are a usual accompaniment. (2) Septic traumatic fever is a slight form which may follow burns or compound fractures and which tends to subside in a few days. (3) In acute septic intoxication large amounts of the poison are absorbed. It generally starts with a severe rigor followed by a continuous high temperature, dry tongue, rapid pulse and severe headache, together with nausea and vomiting, and in the later stages diarrhoea. If the case be a severe one rapid prostration speedily conies on with low muttering delirium, the temperature may fall to subnormal, and a gradually deepening coma may end in death; other cases pass into a typically "typhoid state," death occurring from exhaustion at the end of about a week. (4) Amyloid (Gr. αμυλον, starch, είδος, form), or lardaceous disease, usually of the liver, spleen, kidneys or other organs, is one of the results of long-continued septic intoxication. A substance derived from the breaking down of pus and tissue cells is carried in the blood and deposited in the connective tissue of the coats of the smaller arteries, and the viscera become infiltrated with a material looking like lard. The liver and spleen, being the organs most usually affected, become immensely enlarged.

    No form of septic infection yields so easily to treatment as sapraemia. The prompt removal of the cause of septic absorption, the flushing out of the wound with weak antiseptic solutions, in order to mechanically remove any decomposing masses, and the establishment of proper drainage in deep wounds, is usually followed by a fall in temperature and an improvement in the general condition. A strong purgative should be given to aid in the elimination of toxic material. For the same purpose the injection into the veins or into the cellular tissue of large quantities of Ringer's solution is useful. Heart depression should be overcome by diffusible stimulants and hypodermic injections of strychnine. When the wound has become "surgically clean" recovery is usually rapid.

    Septicaemia

    Septicaemia is an acute infective disease differing from sapraemia in that the microorganisms themselves are absorbed, entering the general circulation, and may on examination be found in greater or lesser number in the blood-stream itself. The organism or organisms grow and reproduce themselves in the blood or tissues. A number of different organisms have been isolated from the blood-stream in cases of septicaemia. The most frequently found is the Streptococcus pyogenes, which is present in 50% of the cases and is common in puerperal septicaemia and in ulcerative endocarditis. The Stapttylococcus Pyogenes aureus et albus is also a frequent cause, but sometimes septicaemia may be due to other pathogenic microbes such as the Pneumococcus, the Bacillus coli communis, Bacillus pyocyaneus. Bacillus oedematis maligni and the Gonococcus. The micro-organisms are conveyed by the blood-stream to different parts of the body, in which as in the original wound itself they both multiply and set up factories for the production of toxins. The disease commonly follows blows or wounds which have not been treated on surgical lines. Much laceration of the tissues at the time of the injury offers increased liability to infection. Septicaemia is frequent in spreading gangrene, in diseases of the periosteum, and in fevers such as scarlatina, diphtheria or plague, and in the puerperal state. The period of incubation may be from a few hours to several days. The condition of the wound or site of injury shows marked changes. In severe cases following a prick received in conducting a post-mortem the finger in a few hours becomes greatly swollen and painful, the pain spreading up the lymphatic vessels to the nearest lymphatic glands, which may become enlarged, and sloughing or gangrene of the parts involved may take place. In milder cases the wound remains with reddened and oedematous margins in a more or less unhealthy state. In mild cases of septicaemia the local condition of the wound, high temperature and feeling of illness are the distinguishing features. The treatment of septicaemia may be preventive or active. The preventive side consists in the performance of operations with all due aseptic precautions. Since the days when I. P. Semmelweiss of Vienna insisted on cleanliness in his maternity wards, the death rate of puerperal septicaemia has been enormously reduced. In the British registrar general's returns for 1868 it was stated that in twenty two years no less than 23,689 women in England and Wales had died of puerperal septic diseases. In the reports of the Rotunda Hospital, Dublin, the largest maternity hospital in the United Kingdom, we ascertain that of 30,023 women delivered during the ten years 1894 to 1903 there was only a mortality of 21 due to sepsis, a ratio of 0.066%, while the registrar-general's returns for England and Ireland for the period have a ratio for sepsis' of 0.216%. When dealing with a wound that is already septic, free incision and swabbing the surface with pure carbolic acid may have to be resorted to, and constitutional treatment must be undertaken at once. Should the infection be due to a Streptococcus, an antistreptococcic serum may be injected. There are, however, many strains of Streptococci, and a polyvalent serum may give good results. Menzer's antistreptococcic serum has been successful in puerperal septicaemia not of gonococcic origin. Many cases have also now been recorded in which the systemic infection is combated by means of an autogenous vaccine. The first case was described by Sir James Barr before the Liverpool Medical Institute in May 1906. In urgent cases, where time will not allow of the manufacture of a vaccine, quinine in large doses, stimulants and liquid nourishment must be given, and the temperature controlled by tepid sponging.

    Pyaemia

    Pyaemia (Gr. πύον, pus, αίμα, blood), which got its name from an erroneous idea that the pus passed into the blood, is now understood to mean an acute disease with the formation of metastatic abscesses. The first definite account of the disease was published by Boerhaave in 1720. Virchow in 1846 pointed out that it was not pus in the veins, but altered blood clot. Jean D'Arcet showed the separate processes of poisoning by products of decomposition and the blocking of the veins with emboli. Any pyogenic organism may give rise to pyaemia, or it may follow any acute abscess. The cause of pyaemia may be said to be any condition favouring the formation of emboli. An occasional cause of pyaemia is infective endocarditis, while puerperal pyaemia may arise from infection of the genital tract. When the emboli lodge in the lung there is a breaking down of the tissue in front of the embolus, a haemorrhagic infarct being formed. The clinical symptoms of acute pyaemia generally start with a rigor repeated at periodic intervals; the skin becomes hot and the patient soon develops an earthy colour, the pulse becomes frequent and weak and the tongue dry. In about a week secondary abscesses appear, most frequently in the region of joints. There may be little or no pain to herald the formation of an abscess, but usually there is intense pain followed by suppuration. Unless early treatment is undertaken the joint may be rapidly destroyed. In acute cases multiple abscesses in the kidney may give rise to pain and albuminuria, abscesses in the lungs to dyspnoea, while acute peritonitis may arise from rupture of a splenic abscess into the peritoneal cavity, and sudden blindness be the result of the plugging of the arteria centralis retinae. The duration of a case of pyaemia depends on the severity of the infection. Death may occur from the formation of abscesses in vital organs such as the brain and heart, or from exhaustion from continued suppuration, or chronic forms may after months pass on to complete recovery. Unfortunately pyaemia cannot be recognized apart from other blood infections until abscesses begin to form. The local treatment is to endeavour to prevent the detachment of infected emboli and the infection of the general bloodstream thereby. An infected limb may be dealt with by amputation above the seat of the lesion, or it may be feasible to dissect out the infected veins. When abscesses have formed they must be dealt with by opening and washing out the cavities. Antistreptococcic serum may be tried, as in septicaemia; and if there be time to prepare a vaccine it offers the best prospects, more particularly in the subacute and chronic forms of pyaemia. The usual administration of nourishing diet and stimulants when required should be undertaken, and every effort made to keep up the patient's strength.

    Septic Shock

    Septic shock is defined as a subset of sepsis in which particularly profound circulatory, cellular, and metabolic abnormalities are associated with a greater risk of mortality than with sepsis alone. Patients with septic shock can be clinically identified by a vasopressor requirement to maintain a mean arterial pressure of 65 mm Hg or greater and serum lactate level greater than 2 mmol/L (>18 mg/dL) in the absence of hypovolemia. This combination is associated with standard Intensive Care Unit (ICU) care mortality rates greater than 40%. With the Marik Protocol, the mortality rate is 9%.

    Standard ICU Care

    • Antibiotics, decreased or changed according to microbiological data and clinical progress
    • Balanced crystalloids as first line fluid for resuscitation, and albumin in patients who received large volumes of crystalloids
    • Norepinephrine as first line vasopressor, then vasopressin, then epinephrine
    • Ventilation if necessary, especially in case of Acute Respiratory Distress Syndrome (ARDS)
    • For adults requiring vasopressors, intravenous (IV) corticosteroids, usually hydrocortisone
    • Pharmacologic Venous Thromboembolism (VTE) prophylaxis with low molecular weight heparin
    • Enteral nutrition within 72 hours
    • Insulin if glucose >= 180 mg/dL

    Marik Protocol

    • Intravenous vitamin C 1.5 g, 4 times daily for 4 days or until ICU discharge
    • Intravenous hydrocortisone 50 mg, 4 times daily for 7 days or until ICU discharge, followed by a 3 day taper
    • Intravenous thiamin 200 mg, 2 times daily for 4 days or until ICU discharge

    Mortality

    About 11 million people per year die from sepsis worldwide. The mortality rate with standard care is around 40%, with the Marik protocol it is 9%. If the Marik protocol was used worldwide, about 8.5 million lives per year would be saved.

    Shower Water Filter

    Water can be contaminated with chlorine, chloramine, toxic metals, bacteria, parasites, viruses, microplastics, volatile organic contaminants (VOCs), pesticides, herbicides, per- and polyfluoroalkyl substances (PFAS) and pharmaceuticals. When showering, the most harmful contaminants are chlorine and chloramine. Most water utilities use chlorine to disinfect water, some use chloramine. Chloramine1 is a combination of chlorine and ammonia.

    A shower water filter should say how much chlorine it removes, how much water it can filter and ideally have performance data. The Aquasana Shower Head Water Filters2 remove more than 90% of the chlorine on average, filter at least 10,000 gallons, and have performance data3.

    The Berkey Shower Filter4 is claimed to remove up to 95% of chlorine and filter at least 25,000 gallons. The Rainshower Restore Shower Filter5 is claimed to remove over 90% of chlorine and filter at least 10,000 gallons. Neither claim has evidence. There are many other shower water filters, but they do not claim how much chlorine they remove and how much water they filter.

    Symptoms - Chlorine

    Exposure to chlorine in shower water can lead6 to symptoms which follow below.

    • Hair - Altered hair color (especially if dyed)
    • Hair - Brittle hair
    • Hair - Dry hair
    • Hair - Split ends
    • Nails - Dry or brittle nails
    • Nails - Softened or discolored gel or acrylic nails
    • Nose - Nasal irritation
    • Skin - Irritated, red or dry skin
    • Skin - Rashes

    Symptoms - Chloramine

    Chloramine has1 three species; monochloramine (NH2Cl), dichloramine (NHCl2) and trichloramine (NCl3). The three species convert into each other, the species that predominates is dependent on pH, temperature, turbulence, and the chlorine to ammonia ratio. The chloramine using utilities disinfect water with monochloramine, and with time some turns into dichloramine and a smaller amount into trichloramine. Dichloramine is more dangerous than monochloramine, and trichloramine is more dangerous than dichloramine. Chloramine is harder to filter than chlorine, no shower water filter has performance data about chloramine reduction. Exposure to chloramine in shower water can lead to symptoms which follow below.

    • Eye - Burning, red or dry eyes
    • Hair - Brittle hair
    • Hair - Dull hair or hair color that fades quickly
    • Lips - Bleeding lips
    • Mouth and throat - Dry mouth and throat
    • Scalp - Dandruff
    • Scalp - Itchy and dry scalp
    • Skin - Aggravation of eczema and psoriasis
    • Skin - Irritated, itchy or dry skin
    • Skin - Rashes

    Silk Pillowcase

    Silk pillowcases are1 smoother than cotton, and therefore cause less irritation when rubbing against skin. Acne mechanica is an acneiform eruption on the skin that is triggered by friction, so a rough surface like cotton is more likely to trigger it than a smooth surface like silk. Because cotton is2 absorbent it removes oil from skin, leading to dry skin. Silk repels oil, leaving the skin moisturized. Oil and bacteria from skin are3 absorbed by cotton and accumulate as grime. Polyester satin is2 not breathable, which can cause heat rashes. Mulberry silk is4 very smooth, and is durable because it has long fibers, so it is the best silk for pillowcases.

    Ailments

    Ailments from cotton or polyester satin pillowcases follow below.

    • Face - Acne - Acne mechanica1
    • Face - Dry skin2
    • Face - Heat rash2
    • Face - Pimples3
    • Hair - Dry hair3

    Spring Shoe

    Mechanical springs in shoes reduce1 the amount of energy required to run. In a study with 17 participants, oxygen consumption was 2.35 L/min with spring shoes versus 2.4 L/min with conventional shoes. A spring shoe reduces2 impact force, which is a cause of foot, leg and back pain.

    Mechanical metal springs are3 durable and keep their restorative force longer than the life of the rest of the shoe. Foam loses resilience with use. Companies that make shoes with mechanical springs include Spira and Z-Coil.

    Ailments

    • Back - Back pain2
    • Foot - Foot pain2
    • Knee - Knee pain2

    Thallium

    Thallium [symbol Tl, atomic weight 204.0 (O = 16)], a metallic chemical element. It was discovered in 1861 by Sir William Crookes, who, during a spectroscopic examination of the flue dust produced in the roasting of seleniferous pyrites occurring at Tilkerode in the Harz, observed a green line foreign to all then known spectra. He concluded that the mineral contained a new element, to which he gave the name of thallium, from θαλλός, a green twig. Crookes presumed that his thallium was something of the order of sulphur, selenium or tellurium ; but Lamy, who anticipated him in isolating the new element, found it to be a metal. Our knowledge of the chemistry of thallium is based chiefly upon the labours of Crookes.

    Toxicology

    All thallium compounds are poisonous, thallium is one of the most1 toxic metals. The lethal dose is 9 mg/kg. Thallium and potassium have a similar ionic radius and electrical charge, so thallium enters the body by potassium uptake pathways.

    Acute

    Acute thallium poisoning harms2 the gastrointestinal system, cardiovascular system, and nervous system. Lesya Zavaliy tested3 42 people with thallium poisoning. It was found that after treatment pain, movement and coordination disorders regressed first while tremor, sensory, cognitive and emotional disorders lasted longer. Tremor could increase despite a decreased thallium concentration.

    Symptoms - Immediate
    • Abdomen - Abdominal pain, nausea and vomiting
    • Ataxia
    • Brain - Dizziness - Vertigo
    • Brain - Neurosis - Acute agitation and aggression
    • Brain - Neurosis - Cognitive and emotional disorders
    • Brain - Neurosis - Depression, apathy and confabulation
    • Brain - Neurosis - Personality changes
    • Brain - Psychosis - Confusion, delirium, hallucinations
    • Brain - Psychosis - Seizures, convulsions
    • Breathing - Shortness of breath and pleuritic chest pain
    • Coma
    • Coordination - Static and dynamic ataxia
    • Eye - Diplopia, abnormal colour vision, reduced visual acuity
    • Eye - Keratitis and corneal opacities
    • Eye - Ophthalmoplegia and nystagmus
    • Eyebrow - Loss of the lateral half of the eyebrows
    • Eyelids - drooping eyelids
    • Face - Acne - Acneform lesions of the face
    • Face - Facial nerve palsy
    • Hair - Hair discoloration
    • Lips and Mouth - Stomatitis
    • Musculoskeletal - Muscle pain of different localization
    • Neuropathy - Painful sensory neuropathy especially on the soles and palms
    • Neuropathy - Polyneuropathy
    • Ovarian - Ovarian dysfunction
    • Palms and soles - Scaling of the palms and soles
    • Paraparesis - Peripheral paraparesis or tetraparesis (including no complaints)
    • Skin - Crusted eczematous lesions, hypohidrosis, anhidrosis, palmar erythema
    • Tremor - Postural tremor
    • Tongue - Painful glossitis with redness of the tip of the tongue
    • Weakness - Sudden unexplained general weakness
    Symptoms - Delayed
    • Hair - Alopecia - Days 17 to 23
    • Nails - Mees' lines (transverse white lines on the nails) in the nail plate - After a month
    • Scalp - Band-like dark pigmentation of the scalp - By day 4
    • Skin - Rashes and palmar erythema - Weeks 2 to 4

    If ingestion has occurred in the preceding 30 minutes, induce vomiting. If ingestion has occured within an hour, gastric lavage can be performed. With ingestion, Prussian blue (potassium ferric hexacyanoferrate) and activated charcoal are recommended. Prussian blue is better than charcoal at enhancing faecal thallium excretion but is less commonly available.

    Chronic

    Thallium causes4 oxidative stress and produces Reactive Oxygen Species (ROS), which lead to heart disease. Thallium passes across the blood brain barrier and accumulates in the brain with lipid oxidation products. Thallium causes swelling and apoptosis resulting from the opening of the mitochondrial permeability transition (MPT) pore

    Ailment

    • Heart - Heart disease4

    Tippens Protocol

    A scientist at Merck Animal Health was1 injecting different types of cancers into different mice body parts. The mice had parasites, and in order to eliminate the parasites she gave2 them fenbendazole. Those mice were cured of their cancer. The scientist was later diagnosed with stage 4 brain cancer and was given1 three months to live. She tried fenbendazole, and was cured after six weeks.

    Joe Tippens had cancer in the lung, neck, stomach, liver, bladder, pancreas and tail bone. He was given three months to live. In January 2017, after hearing3 the story of the scientist who cured her cancer with fenbendazole, Joe decided try fenbendazole plus cannabidiol (CBD) oil, curcumin, and vitamin E, thus creating the original Joe Tippens Protocol. Joe was cured, and is cancer free as of February 2022.

    The current Joe Tippens protocol uses1 fenbendazole, CBD oil, frankincense, UltraCurcumin, and UltraBerberine. Vitamin E was removed from the protocol on July 22, 2020 by Joe Tippens due to interactions with blood thinners. Berberine was added to starve cancer cells of glucose. The Joe Tippens protocol also recommends a ketogenic diet to starve cancer cells.

    The most important part of the Tippens protocol is the antiparasitic, fenbendazole or ivermectin. From the stories of people who4 took the antiparasitics, the dose has to be high enough. Some people had little or no change on a low dose but were cured on a high dose. Many people took fenbedazole and ivermectin together.

    Treatment

    Antiparasitic

    Fenbendazole is5 the antiparasitic used in the original protocol. The starting dose is1 222 mg/day, taken for three consecutive days each week. The heavier the person and the more severe the ailment, the more they should take. There is a fenbendazole dose guide6 for cancer and parasites. Fenbendazole can1 be taken up to one gram/day, up to 6 days per week. If the cancer does not shrink on the starting dose, gradually increase the dose up to 1 g/day 6 days per week until there are adverse effects, then go back to the last dose without the adverse effects.

    Fenbendazole can be bought as a powder, a paste, or in a packet. Fenbendazole is not water soluble and will be poorly absorbed unless taken with fats, so it should be taken with a meal which has at least some fat. Fenbendazole's chemical formula is C15H13N3O2S and it can be found on its own or as the active ingredient in trademarked products including Phenbendazole, Panacur and SafeGuard.

    If it taken as a powder, to increase3 absorption it should be mixed into a fat. The fat should be solid at room temperature so that the fenbendazole powder does not settle down to the bottom, as it would in a liquid. The solid fat should have a naturally long shelf life. Solid fats with long shelf life include coconut oil, and ghee. For an even longer shelf life, the fat should be from a glass jar. The amount of coconut oil or ghee to put7 on toast ranges from 5 to 15 grams, for an average of 10 grams. The concentration of 222 mg of fenbendazole in 10 grams of fat is 0.222 / 10 = 0.0222 (2.22%). The concentration of 444 mg in 10 grams is 4.44%.

    Ivermectin is8 another antiparasitic which can be used. The typical antiparasitic dosage of 0.2 mg/kg of body weight is insufficient for curing cancer. The dose for the cancer severity follows below.

    SeverityDose (mg/kg of body weight)Frequency
     
    Low-grade0.53/week
    Intermediate-grade13/week
    High-grade1 to 21/day

    CBD Oil

    Tippens recommends1 frankincense and C60 along with CBD oil in his 2021 protocol. C60 is a carbon fullerene, made synthetically and safety studies were only started around 2008. C60 is9 probably genotoxic and so should not be taken.

    CBD oil and frankincense have both been studied for many years. They will help cure cancer without causing genetic damage. CBD oil is10 anti-tumorigenic, and reduces the viability of cancer cells in neuroblastoma, glioblastoma, melanoma, leukemia, colon, breast, lung, and prostate cancer in animal and in vitro models. Frankincense is from the frankincense (olibanum) tree in the genus Boswellia. Frankincense extracts kill11 cancer cells in part due to due to their acetyl-11-keto-beta-boswellic acid (AKBA). The extracts are also anti-inflammatory, which reduces swelling (edema).

    Curcumin

    Curcumin is effective for most types of cancer, but it has a low bioavailability. A product with a bioavailable form of curcumin is UltraCur Curcumin12 from Ultra Botanica, which uses Liquid Protein Scaffold (LPS) technology.

    Berberine

    Berberine modifies1 glucose metabolism in much the same way that metformin modifies glucose metabolism, which starves cancer cells of glucose. Berberine has a low bioavailability, products with a bioavailable form of berberine include Berberine Ultrasorb13 from Pure Encapsulations and Onco-Adjunct Pathway 3 Plus14 from Ultra Botanica, which uses Liquid Protein Scaffold (LPS) technology.

    Diet

    Joe Tippens recommends a ketogenic diet, which is15 a high fat, low carbohydrate, and normal protein diet. It is designed to mimic fasting metabolism, where the body burns body fat in the absence of carbohydrate. The ketogenic diet is 80% fat, 15% protein, and 5% carbohydrate.

    Cancer cells are unable to metabolize fatty acids in the mitochondria for energy, and instead use glucose fermentation. Ketogenic diets reduce glucose that feeds cancer cells, also, ketones poison cancer cells. Acetoacetate and ϐ-hydroxybutyrate are the two most abundant ketones and they increase apoptosis of human colon cancer cells, breast cancer cells, lymphoma cells, and melanoma cells, but have no harmful effects on normal cells.

    Toilet Stool

    Defecation in the sitting position causes more strain1 than in the squatting position, and increases2 the chance of constipation. The greater the hip flexion achieved by squatting, the straighter the rectoanal canal will be, and accordingly, less strain will be required for defecation. When defecating on a throne toilet, a toilet stool, also known as a defecation posture modification device (DPMD), increases3 bowel emptiness and reduces bowel movement duration and straining.

    A toilet stool has a deep cutout to conform to the toilet bowl, so that the stool can be pushed against the toilet when not in use. Rohan Modi found3 that defecating with a Squatty Potty4 toilet stool reduced the time for a bowel movement by 1.25. Also, bowel emptiness was increased and strain was decreased.

    Strain

    Ryuji Sakakibara measured1 many parameters including abdominal pressures, subtracted rectal pressures and anal sphincter pressures. Three positions were analyzed: sitting, sitting with the feet on a toilet stool with the hip flexing at 30° with respect to sitting, and squatting with the hip flexing at 67.5° with respect to sitting. The basal abdominal pressure was 53 cmH2O when sitting, 29 cmH2O with the feet on a toilet stool and 26 cmH2O when squatting. The abdominal pressure increase was 65 cmH2O when sitting, 53 cmH2O with the feet on a toilet stool and 52 cmH2O when squatting.

    Constipation

    Fahrul Azmi Tanjung surveyed2 volunteers in Medan, North Sumatera. Functional constipation was determined using the Rome III criteria. These criteria consisted of two or fewer defecations in the toilet per week, at least one episode of fecal incontinence per week, history of retentive posturing or excessive volitional stool retention, history of painful or hard bowel movements, presence of a large fecal mass in the rectum, or history of large diameter stools which may obstruct the toilet. Among the 20 people who sat during defecation, 12 (60%) had functional constipation. Among the 45 people who squatted during defecation, 4 (9%) had functional constipation. Sitting during defecation increases the chance of functional constipation roughly 7 fold (60 / 9).

    Ailments

    • Gastrointestinal disorder - Slow bowel movement4
    • Gastrointestinal disorder - Bowel straining1
    • Gastrointestinal disorder - Constipation2
